تحقیق: تحقیقی جواب کا بیان ہے جس کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ تم نے قید کا معنی ہی غلط کیا ہے تم نے کہا کہ قید کہتے ہیں کہ عوارض ذاتیہ کا موضوع کیلئے ثبوت حیثیت کے واسطہ سے ہوگا یہ منی غلط ہے بلکہ صحیح معنی یہ ہے کہ علم میں جوعوارض مبحوث عنہا ہوں گے ان سے بحث کے وقت اس حیثیت کا لحاظ ہو گا یعنی یہ بحث کی قید ہے آگے عوارض عام ہیں ان کا ثبوت بالواسطہ ہو یا بلا واسطہ بعنوان دیگر یہاں پر چیزیں دو ہیں ایک یہ ہے کہ حیثیت قید ہو عوارض کے ثبوت کی اور دوسری یہ کہ حیثیت قید ہو عوارض ذاتیہ سے بحث کی اور یہی معنی ہے حیثیت بیانیہ کا پس شاریخ کی کلام لوٹ گئی صاحب توضیح کے کلام کی طرف گویا کہ صاحب تلویح نے اسی معنی کو بیان کیا جس کو صاحب توضیح نے بیان کیا۔ واللہ اعلم بالصواب۔
One thing occurs to another as an accident; it is necessary that it precedes the accident. For example, health and illness are among the things that attach to the human body in respect that it is healthy and ill, and movement and stillness are not among those that attach to the body in respect that it moves and is still. The famous response to this is that what is intended is the possibility of health and illness or the possibility of movement and stillness, and its capacity, and this is not among the attributes of the investigated subject in the science. And the investigation is that since the subject is defined in terms of the essential attributes of what is investigated in the science, it is restricted with respect to 'aspect' (haythiyya), building on the meaning that the investigation of attributes is according to the aspect and by looking towards the aspect—that is, consideration is given in all investigations to this universal meaning, not to the meaning that all attributes of the investigated attach to the subject by means of this aspect definitively.
In the case of making the aspect a restriction, does the self-predication objection not follow?
Yes: The commentator said that by making the aspect a restriction, the previous objection does not apply to us, but another famous objection applies: that when we make the aspect a restriction, the meaning of the restriction is that we investigate those essential attributes that attach to the subject by means of the aspect. This would necessitate that in the science of medicine, the investigation of health and illness themselves is not correct; similarly in natural science, movement and stillness; and in astronomy, shape itself—because this would lead to the corruption of self-predication. For if the meaning of the restriction is that the science investigates essential attributes that attach to the thing by means of the aspect, then the essential attributes are the mediated (dhu'l-wasita) and the aspect is the mediator. The rule is that the mediator precedes and the mediated is posterior. Thus self-predication arises, which is invalid. For example, if in medicine we investigate health and illness, then health and illness become among the essential attributes, and essential attributes are mediated because they attach to the body by means of health and illness. Thus health and illness are posterior as mediated and prior as mediator—this is self-predication. Similarly in astronomy, if shape is investigated, then shape becomes among the essential attributes, and essential attributes are mediated because they attach to the body by means of shape—thus shape is posterior as mediated and prior as mediator; this is self-predication. Similarly in natural science, if movement and stillness are investigated, then movement and stillness become among essential attributes, and the remaining essential attributes are mediated because they attach to bodies by means of movement and stillness. Thus movement and stillness are posterior as mediated and prior as mediator—this is self-predication. It becomes like one who fled from the rain and stood under the spout. The famous response to this well-known objection is that here there are two distinct things: the essence of health and the essence of illness, and the possibility of health and the possibility of illness; similarly, the essence of movement, the essence of stillness, the essence of shape, and the possibility of movement, the possibility of stillness, and the possibility of shape. Given this, the aspect which is the mediator is the possibility of health, the possibility of illness, the possibility of movement, the possibility of stillness, the possibility of shape; and the mediated are the essence of health, the essence of illness, the essence of movement, the essence of stillness, and the essence of shape. Thus the mediated is one thing and the mediator is another, so self-predication does not arise.
Investigation: This is an exposition of the investigative response, the gist of which is that you have misunderstood the meaning of restriction. You said that restriction means that essential attributes are established for the subject by means of the aspect. This is incorrect. Rather the correct meaning is that in the science, when investigating the attributes that are investigated, the aspect is taken into account—i.e., it is a restriction on the investigation. The attributes are general; their establishment may be mediated or unmediated under another heading. There are two things: one is that the aspect is a restriction on the establishment of the attributes, and the other is that the aspect is a restriction on the investigation of essential attributes. This is the meaning of the 'declarative aspect' (al-haythiyya al-bayaniyya). Thus the words of the commentator revert to the words of the author of al-Tawdih, as if the author of al-Talwih expressed the same meaning that the author of al-Tawdih expressed. And Allah knows best what is correct.
Source pages p. 406

Commentary
This passage addresses a well-known objection in usul al-fiqh concerning the definition of a science's subject (mawdu') and its essential attributes (a'rad dhatiyya). The objection is that if the investigation is restricted with respect to an 'aspect' (haythiyya), then the aspect itself becomes a mediator, and the attribute under investigation (like health in medicine) would be both mediated and mediator, leading to self-predication (taqaddum al-shay' 'ala nafsihi). The text presents two responses. The famous response distinguishes between the essence of the attribute (e.g., health itself) and its possibility (e.g., the possibility of health). The aspect then refers to the possibility, not the essence, so the essence is the mediated and the possibility is the mediator—two different things, avoiding self-predication. The investigative response (tahqiq) corrects the understanding of the restriction: the restriction is not on the attribution itself but on the investigation—i.e., the aspect is a restriction on the mode of inquiry. The attributes are not required to attach to the subject via the aspect; rather, the aspect is just a perspective for investigation. Hence self-predication does not arise. This analysis is crucial for correctly defining a science's subject and avoiding logical contradictions.

Key Terms
حیثیت (haythiyya): The 'aspect' or 'perspective' that qualifies the investigation of essential attributes in a science.
تقدم الشئی علی نفسہ (taqaddum al-shay' 'ala nafsihi): A logical fallacy where a thing is considered prior to itself (self-predication).
ذوالواسطہ (dhu'l-wasita): The mediated item – the attribute that is attached to the subject via an intermediary.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the investigative response (tahqiq), what does the aspect (haythiyya) restrict?
A) The establishment of essential attributes on the subject
B) The investigation of essential attributes
C) The definition of the subject's essence
D) The possibility of attributes

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi resolution of self-predication distinguishes between the aspect as a perspective for investigation and the aspect as a causal mediator, ensuring sound definitions of scientific subjects.

One Subject Can Serve Multiple Sciences
Asserting that a single entity can be the subject of different sciences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a single entity can be the subject of multiple sciences according to Hanafi usul, and identify the types of attributes that enable this.

Core Idea
A single subject can host multiple sciences because its diverse attributes provide distinct angles of investigation.

In usul al-fiqh, the definition of a science includes its subject matter (mawdu'). A common claim is that a single topic cannot belong to two sciences. The commentator challenges this, using the argument that a single entity has diverse attributes. This lesson unpacks that argument and shows how it applies to the classification of Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial claim and its refutation
Start by noting the well-known view that a single thing cannot be the subject of two sciences. Then see how the commentator immediately rejects this as both possible and actual.
This sets up the key logical tension: common assumption vs. rational analysis.
2
Grasp the concept of diverse attributes (a'rad mutanawwi'a)
Understand that any real entity has multiple attributes: real (haqiqiyya), relative (idafiyya), affirmative (ijabiyya), and negative (salbiyya). Each science investigates a subset of these.
The commentator uses the example of human body studied in medicine, physics, and astronomy.
3
Recognize that the truly single entity has no parts
The text argues that al-wahid al-haqiqi (the truly one) has no parts; therefore attributes attach directly to its essence, not via components. This avoids infinite regress.
This is a metaphysical grounding: unity of the subject does not prevent multiplicity of investigated attributes.
4
Apply to classification of Islamic sciences
For instance, the Quran is studied in tafsir (meaning), fiqh (rulings), and usul (derivation). Each focuses on different attributes of the same revealed text.
This shows the practical relevance of the principle for organizing knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمِنْهَا أَنَّ الْمَشْهُورَ اَنَّ الشَّيْءَ الْوَاحِدَ لَا يَكُونُ مَوْضُوعًا لِلْعِلْمَيْنِ أَقُولُ هَذَا غَيْرُ مُمْتَنِعٍ بَلْ وَاقِعٌ فَإِنَّ الشَّيءَ الْوَاحِدَ يَكُونُ لَهُ أَعْرَاضٌ مُتَنَوّعَةٌ فَفِي كُلّ عِلْمٍ يُبْحَثُ عَنْ بَعْض مِنْهَا كَمَا ذَكَرُنَا.
وَإِنَّمَا قُلْنَا إِنَّ الشَّيْء الْوَاحِدَ يَكُونُ لَهُ أَعْرَاضٌ مُتَنَوِّعَةٌ فَإِنَّ الْوَاحِدَ الْحَقِيقِيَّ يُوصَفُ بِصِفَاتٍ كَثِيرَةٍ ، وَلَا يَضُرُّ أَن يَكُونَ بَعْضُهَا حَقِيقِيَّةً وَبَعْضُهَا إِضَافِيَّةٌ وَبَعْضُهَا إِيجَابِيَّةٌ وَبَعْضُهَا سَلْبِيَّةٌ ، وَلَا شَيْءٍ مِنْهَا يَلْحَقُهُ لِجُزْئِهِ لِعَدَمِ الْجُزْءُ لَهُ فَلُحُوقُ بَعْضِهَا لَا بُدَّ أَنْ يَكُونَ لِذَاتِهِ قَطْعًا لِلتَّسَلْسُلِ فِي الْمَبْدَأُ فَلُحُوقُ الْبَعْضِ
And among them is that the well-known view is that a single thing cannot be the subject of two sciences. I say: this is not impossible; rather it occurs. For a single thing can have diverse attributes, and in each science, some of them are investigated, as we have mentioned.
We only say that a single thing has diverse attributes because the truly single entity is described by many attributes. It does not harm that some of them are real (haqiqiyya), some relative (idafiyya), some affirmative (ijabiyya), and some negative (salbiyya). None of them attach to it due to its part, for it has no part. So the attachment of some of them must necessarily be to its essence, to stop infinite regress in the principle. So the attachment of some...
Source pages p. 407

Commentary
This passage presents a rule from the commentary: it is well-known that one thing cannot be the subject (mawdu') of two different sciences. The commentator (probably the author of al-Talwih or al-Tawdih) refutes this, stating it is not impossible but rather actually occurs. The reasoning is that a single entity (al-shay' al-wahid) possesses multiple diverse attributes (a'rad). Each science investigates a subset of these attributes. For example, the human body is studied in medicine (health and illness), physics (motion and rest), and astronomy (shape), among others. The passage further argues that the truly one entity (al-wahid al-haqiqi) can be described by many attributes of different types: real (like physical properties), relative (like relations), affirmative (like existence), and negative (like absence of something). Since it has no parts, these attributes must attach directly to its essence, not via parts, to avoid infinite regress. This establishes that a single subject can legitimately be studied by multiple sciences, each focusing on different essential attributes. This is important for understanding the classification of sciences in the Islamic tradition.

Key Terms
موضوع (mawdu'): The subject matter of a science; the entity about which the science investigates its essential attributes.
اعراض متنوعة (a'rad mutanawwi'a): Diverse attributes (real, relative, affirmative, negative) that belong to a single subject and are investigated in different sciences.
الواحد الحقيقي (al-wahid al-haqiqi): The truly one entity that has no parts; it can be described by multiple attributes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the proof that a single thing can be the subject of two sciences?
A) The thing has parts, each studied by a different science
B) The thing possesses diverse attributes, each science investigating some of them
C) The thing changes over time
D) The thing is abstract

Key Takeaway
A single subject can be investigated by multiple sciences because it possesses diverse attributes, each science focusing on a subset.

One Thing as Subject of Multiple Sciences
Rational proof for the possibility of a single subject of multiple sciences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle that a single entity can be the subject of multiple sciences by identifying the essential attributes that distinguish each science.

Core Idea
Multiple sciences can study the same thing if each focuses on a different essential attribute.

This passage tackles a foundational question in Hanafi usul al-fiqh: can one and the same thing be the subject of more than one science? The author argues it is both possible and actual, using a rational proof and the concrete example of astronomy (hay'a) and natural philosophy (tabi'iyya), which both study the bodies of the world but under different aspects. Understanding this principle sharpens your ability to define the boundaries of different Islamic sciences, including fiqh and usul.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
The author asserts that it is possible for one thing to be the subject of two sciences, and that this actually occurs.
The claim includes both possibility and actuality.
2
Examine the rational proof (dalil aqli)
The proof is that the unity or difference of two sciences depends on the unity or difference of their issues (masa'il), and issues are unified or differentiated both by their subjects (mawdu'at) and their predicates (mahmulat). Since predicates refer to attributes, different attributes can give rise to different sciences from the same subject.
The key phrase: 'issues are unified and differ according to their predicates, which refer back to those attributes.'
3
Analyze the example
Astronomy (science of the heavens) and natural philosophy both study the bodies of the world, but astronomy investigates them insofar as they have shape, while natural philosophy investigates them insofar as they have nature. The subject is the same; the predicate (attribute) differs.
The aspect (haythiyya) clarifies the direction of investigation, not the subject itself.
4
Understand the role of haythiyya
The aspect is not part of the subject; it indicates which attribute is being studied. If it were part, each science would study that aspect itself, but they study what attaches to it.
This refutes the objection that the aspect changes the subject.
5
Apply to Islamic sciences
For example, the act of prayer (salat) is the subject of both fiqh (studying its rulings) and usul al-fiqh (studying its evidentiary derivation). The essential attribute differs: fiqh looks at practical application, usul at proof structure.
This shows the principle's relevance within Hanafi tradition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الْآخَرِ إنْ كَانَ لِذَاتِهِ فَهُوَ الْمَطْلُوبُ وَإِنْ كَانَ لِغَيْرِهِ نَتَكَلَّمُ فِي ذَلِكَ الْغَيْرِ حَتَّى يَنْتَهِيَ إِلَى ذَاتِهِ قَطْعًا لِلتَّسَلُسُلِ فِي الْمَبْدَأِ . وَلَأَنَّهُ يَلْزَمُ اسْتِكْمَالُهُ مِنْ غَيْرِهِ إِذَا ثَبَتَ ذَلِكَ يُمْكِنُ أَنْ يَكُونَ الشَّيْءُ الْوَاحِدُ مَوْضُوعَ عِلْمَيْنِ وَيَكُونَ تَمْيِيزُهُمَا بِحَسَبِ الْأَعْرَاضِ الْمَبْحُوثِ عَنْهَا وَذَلِكَ؛ لِأَنَّ اتِّحَادَ الْعِلْمَيْنِ وَاخْتِلَافَهُمَا بِحَسَبِ اتِّحَادِ الْمَعْلُومَاتِ وَاخْتِلَافِهَا وَالْمَعْلُومَاتُ هِيَ الْمَسَائِلُ فَكَمَا أَنَّ الْمَسَائِلَ تَتَّحِدُ وَتَخْتَلِفُ بِحَسَبِ مَوْضُوعَاتِهَا وَهِيَ رَاجِعَةٌ إِلَى مَوْضُوعِ الْعِلْمِ فَكَذَلِكَ تَتَّحِدُ الْمَسَائِلُ وَتَخْتَلِفُ بِحَسَبِ مَحْمُولَاتِهَا وَهِيَ رَاجِعَةٌ إِلَى تِلْكَ الْأَعْرَاضِ وَإِنْ أُرِيدَ أَنَّ الِاصْطِلَاحَ جَرَى بِأَنَّ الْمَوْضُوعَ مُعْتَبَرٌ فِي ذَلِكَ لَا الْمَحْمُولَ فَحِينَئِذٍ لَا مُشَاحَّةَ فِي ذَلِكَ عَلَى أَنَّ قَوْلَهُمْ: إِنَّ مَوْضُوعَ الْهَيْئَةِ هِيَ أَجْسَامُ الْعَالَمِ مِنْ حَيْثُ لَهَا شَكْلٌ وَمَوْضُوعُ عِلْمِ السَّمَاءِ وَالْعَالَمِ مِنَ الطَّبِيعِيِّ أَجْسَامُ الْعَالَمِ مِنْ حَيْثُ لَهَا طَبِيعِيَّةٌ قَوْلٌ بِأَنَّ مَوْضُوعَهُمَا وَاحِدٌ لَكِنَّ اخْتِلَافَهُمَا بِاخْتِلَافِ الْمَحْمُولِ؛ لِأَنَّ الْحَيْثِيَّةَ فِيهِمَا بَيَانُ الْبُحُوثِ عَنْهُ لَا أَنَّهَا جُزْءُ الْمَوْضُوعِ وَإِلَّا لَزِمَ أَنْ لَا يُبْحَثَ فِيهِمَا عَنْ هَاتَيْنِ الْحَيْثِيَّتَيْنِ، بَلْ عَمَّا يَلْحَقُهُمَا لِهَاتَيْنِ الْحَيْثِيَّتَيْنِ وَالْوَاقِعُ خِلَافُ ذَلِكَ، وَاللهُ أَعْلَمُ.
The other: if it is due to itself, then it is what is sought; and if it is due to something else, we will discuss that something else until it reaches its own self, cutting off infinite regress in the beginning. And because it necessarily requires completion from something else. When that is established, it is possible that one thing be the subject of two sciences, and their distinction be according to the attributes investigated. That is because the unity and difference of the two sciences is according to the unity and difference of the known objects, and the known objects are the issues. So just as issues are unified and differ according to their subjects and they refer back to the subject of the science, so too issues are unified and differ according to their predicates and they refer back to those attributes. And if it is meant that convention has it that the subject is considered in that, not the predicate, then there is no dispute in that. However, their statement that the subject of [the science of] astronomy is the bodies of the world insofar as they have shape, and the subject of the science of the heavens and the world from natural [science] is the bodies of the world insofar as they have a nature, is a statement that their subject is one, but their difference is due to the difference of the predicate; because the aspect in both is a clarification of the investigations about it, not that it is a part of the subject. Otherwise, it would necessitate that they do not investigate in the two sciences these two aspects, but rather what attaches to them due to these two aspects, but the reality is contrary to that. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 408

Commentary
This passage is from a commentary on Al-Tawdih and Al-Talwih, discussing the third topic regarding the subject (mawdu') of a science. The author argues against the majority view that one thing cannot be the subject of multiple sciences. He makes two claims: (1) it is possible, and (2) it actually occurs. The rational proof is that a single entity can have different types of essential attributes (a'radh dhatiyya). For example, a human body can be studied in medicine (as a physical body) and in psychology (as a thinking being). The author uses the example of the sciences of astronomy (hay'a) and natural philosophy (tabi'iyya), which both study the bodies of the world but under different aspects: shape versus nature. The subject is the same, but the predicate (the attribute studied) differs, making them distinct sciences. The commentary clarifies that the 'aspect' (haythiyya) is not part of the subject but indicates the direction of inquiry. The passage also discusses infinite regress and the need to trace a quality back to its source in the essence.

Key Terms
مَوْضُوع (mawdu'): The subject of a science; that which a science studies in terms of its essential attributes.
عَرَض ذَاتِيّ (arad dhati): An essential attribute or accident that inheres in a thing necessarily or by its nature, not accidentally.
حَيْثِيَّة (haythiyya): The aspect or perspective under which something is considered, indicating the direction of inquiry rather than being a constituent of the subject.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which two sciences are given as an example of sharing the same subject but differing in the attribute studied?
A) Medicine and psychology
B) Astronomy (hay'a) and natural philosophy (tabi'iyya)
C) Physics and chemistry
D) Logic and grammar

Key Takeaway
One thing can be the subject of multiple sciences when each science investigates a different essential attribute (arad dhati), and the aspect (haythiyya) merely clarifies the direction of inquiry without altering the subject.

Differentiating Sciences by Essential Attributes
Discusses differentiation of sciences by predicates despite shared subject

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how two sciences can share the same subject matter yet be distinguished by the essential attributes they investigate, and apply this principle to differentiate usul al-fiqh from other Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Sciences sharing a subject are distinguished by the essential attributes they investigate.

How do we define a distinct science? In the Islamic intellectual tradition, sciences are often distinguished by their subject matter. However, a single subject can be studied from multiple perspectives, each focusing on different inherent characteristics. This passage from a commentary on al-Tawḍīḥ discusses a finer point: sciences can share the same subject but be distinguished by the predicates (maḥmūlāt) they examine. Understanding this helps clarify the boundaries of Islamic sciences, especially how usul al-fiqh relates to fiqh itself.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core claim
Read the first few lines: 'Essential attributes that are diverse... so the two sciences or the sciences are distinguished by the attributes investigated, even if the subject is the same.' Pinpoint the central thesis.
The claim is that subject-matter unity does not preclude differentiation.
2
Understand the reasoning
The author argues that the unity or diversity of a science depends on the problems (masāʾil), which are unified either by subject or by predicate. If predicates revert to a single type of essential attribute, they unify; if they differ, they separate.
This extends the usual criterion of subject alone.
3
Examine the example
The author provides an example: astronomy (ʿilm al-hayʾa) and physics (ʿilm al-samāʾ wa al-ʿālam) both study the bodies of the world, but astronomy investigates their shapes while physics investigates their natures.
The same subject, different essential attributes, hence two sciences.
4
Apply to usul al-fiqh
Consider usul al-fiqh and fiqh: both deal with acts of mukallaf (subject). Usul focuses on evidence and derivation of rulings (essential attribute: istidlāl), while fiqh focuses on the rulings themselves (essential attribute: itself the ḥukm). They are distinct sciences despite shared subject.
This illustrates the principle in a field relevant to this course.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذَاتِيَّةٌ مُتَنَوِّعَةٌ أَى مُخْتَلِفَةٌ بِالنَّوْعِ يُبْحَثُ فِي عِلْمٍ عَنْ بَعْضِ أَنْوَاعِهَا ، وَفِي عِلْمٍ آخَرَ عَنْ بَعْضٍ آخَرَ فَيَتَمَايَزُ الْعِلْمَانِ أَوِ الْعُلُومُ بِالْأَعْرَاضِ الْمَبْحُوثِ عَنْهَا ، وَإِنْ اتَّحَدَ الْمَوْضُوعُ ، وَذَلِكَ ، لِأَنَّ اتِّحَادَ الْعِلْمِ وَاخْتِلَافَهُ إِنَّمَا هُوَ بِحَسَبِ الْمَعْلُومَاتِ أَعْنِي الْمَسَائِلَ ، وَ كَمَا تَتَّحِدُ الْمَسَائِلُ بِاتِّحَادِ مَوْضُوعَاتِهَا بِأَنْ يَرْجِعَ الْجَمِيعُ إِلَى مَوْضُوعِ الْعِلْمِ وَتَخْتَلِفُ بِاخْتِلَافِهَا كَذَلِكَ تَتَّحِدُ بِاتِّحَادِ مَحْمُولَاتِهَا بِأَنْ يَرْجِعَ الْجَمِيعُ إِلَى نَوْعٍ مِنْ الْأَعْرَاضِ الذَّاتِيَّةِ لِلْمَوْضُوعِ وَتَخْتَلِفُ بِاخْتِلَافِهَا فَكَمَا اُعْتُبِرَ اخْتِلَافُ الْعُلُومِ بِاخْتِلَافِ الْمَوْضُوعَاتِ يَجُوزُ أَنْ يُعْتَبَرَ بِاخْتِلَافِ الْمَحْمُولَاتِ بِأَنْ يُؤْخَذَ مَوْضُوعٌ وَاحِدٌ بِالذَّاتِ وَالِاعْتِبَارِ وَيُجْعَلَ الْبَحْثُ عَنْ بَعْضِ أَعْرَاضِهِ الذَّاتِيَّةِ عِلْمًا وَعَنْ الْبَعْضِ الْآخَرِ عِلْمًا آخَرَ فَيَكُونَانِ عِلْمَيْنِ مُتَشَارِكَيْنِ فِي الْمَوْضُوعِ مُتَمَايِزَيْنِ فِي الْمَحْمُولِ . وَأَمَّا الْوُقُوعُ فَلِأَنَّهُمْ جَعَلُوا أَجْسَامَ الْعَالَمِ وَهِيَ الْبَسَائِطُ مَوْضُوعَ عِلْمِ الْهَيْئَةِ مِنْ حَيْثُ الشَّكْلِ وَمَوْضُوعَ عِلْمِ السَّمَاءِ وَالْعَالَمِ مِنْ حَيْثُ الطَّبِيعِيَّةِ وَالْحَيْثِيَّةُ فِيهِمَا بَيَانُ الْأَعْرَاضِ الذَّاتِيَّةِ الْمَبْحُوثِ عَنْهَا أَجْزَاء الْمَوْضُوعِ وَإِلَّا لَمَا وَقَعَ الْبَحْثُ عَنْهَا فِي الْعِلْمَيْنِ فَمَوْضُوعُ كُلِّ مِنْهُمَا أَجْسَامُ الْعَالَمِ عَلَى الْإِطْلَاقِ إِلَّا أَنَّ الْبَحْثَ فِي الْهَيْئَةِ عَنْ أَشْكَالِهَا ، وَفِي السَّمَاءِ وَالْعَالَمِ عَنْ طَبَائِعِهَا فَهُمَا عِلْمَانِ مُخْتَلِفَانِ بِاخْتِلَافِ مَحْمُولَاتِ الْمَسَائِلِ مَعَ اتِّحَادِ الْمَوْضُوعِ
Essential attributes that are diverse, i.e., different in kind: in one science some of its types are investigated, and in another science other types, so the two sciences or the sciences are distinguished by the attributes investigated, even if the subject is the same. That is because the unity and difference of a science is only according to the known things, i.e., the problems. And just as problems are unified by the unity of their subjects, such that all revert to the subject of the science, and differ by their diversity, likewise they are unified by the unity of their predicates, such that all revert to a type of essential attributes of the subject, and differ by their diversity. So just as the difference of sciences is considered by the difference of subjects, it is permissible that it be considered by the difference of predicates, such that one subject is taken in essence and consideration, and the investigation of some of its essential attributes is made one science, and of the other attributes another science. Thus they become two sciences sharing the subject and distinguishing by the predicate. As for the actual occurrence, it is because they made the bodies of the world, which are the simple elements, the subject of astronomy from the perspective of shape, and the subject of the science of the sky and the world from the perspective of nature. The 'perspective' in them is the explanation of the essential attributes investigated concerning the parts of the subject; otherwise the investigation of them would not occur in the two sciences. So the subject of each of them is the bodies of the world absolutely, except that the investigation in astronomy is about their shapes, and in the science of the sky and the world about their natures. Thus they are two different sciences due to the difference of the predicates of the problems, despite the unity of the subject.
Source pages p. 412

Commentary
This passage is a methodological discussion on how sciences are differentiated. The author argues that sciences can be distinguished not only by their subject matter (mawḍūʿ) but also by the specific essential attributes (aʿrāḍ dhātiyya) they investigate. Even if two sciences share the same subject, they may be considered separate because they focus on different types of essential attributes of that subject. He provides supporting examples: astronomy (ʿilm al-hayʾa) and physics (ʿilm al-samāʾ wa al-ʿālam) both study the bodies of the world, but astronomy investigates their shapes, while physics investigates their natures. Therefore, they are distinct sciences despite sharing the same subject. This principle is important in usul al-fiqh for defining the scope of legal theory and distinguishing it from other Islamic sciences.

Key Terms
عَرَضٌ ذَاتِيٌّ (ʿaraḍ dhātī): An attribute that necessarily follows the subject as part of its quiddity, used to differentiate sciences
مَوْضُوعُ الْعِلْمِ (mawḍūʿ al-ʿilm): The subject matter of a science, that which is investigated in its problems
مَحْمُولُ الْمَسْأَلَةِ (maḥmūl al-masʾala): The predicate or attribute affirmed of the subject in a given problem of a science

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how can two sciences be different even if they share the same subject matter?
A) By having different methods of inquiry
B) By investigating different essential attributes of the same subject
C) By using different terminology
D) By being from different historical periods

Key Takeaway
The unity or diversity of a science depends on both subject and predicate; sharing a subject does not preclude being a distinct science if the investigated essential attributes differ.

One Thing as Subject of Multiple Sciences: Possibility and Actuality
Argues for possibility and actuality of one entity being subject of multiple sciences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two claims made by the matn author regarding a single entity being the subject of multiple sciences and explain the rational proof (dalil aqli) for each claim.

Core Idea
A single entity can be subject of multiple sciences when each science investigates a different type of essential attribute.

This passage addresses a foundational question in usul al-fiqh: can one entity, like human actions, be the subject of multiple disciplines (fiqh, usul, kalam)? The commentator argues that it is both possible and actual, based on the concept of essential attributes (ʿawāriḍ dhātiyya). We will dissect this argument and its implications for the classification of Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two claims
The matn author makes two claims: (a) it is possible in essence for one thing to be the subject of multiple sciences, and (b) this actually occurs in reality (actuality). Read the passage to locate these claims.
The first claim is about possibility (jawāz), the second about actuality (wuqūʿ).
2
Analyze the proof for possibility
The commentator argues that sciences are distinguished by the types of essential attributes they investigate. If one thing has multiple types of essential attributes, different sciences can each investigate one type. The unity or multiplicity of a science depends on whether its issues revert to one type or multiple types of essential attributes.
This is a rational argument (dalil aqli) based on the structure of knowledge.
3
Analyze the proof for actuality
The example of astronomy (ʿilm al-hayʾa) and the science of the heavens (ʿilm al-samāʾ) is given. Both study celestial bodies, but astronomy studies them in terms of shape, while the science of the heavens studies them in terms of nature. The aspect (ḥaythiyya) is not part of the subject but an explanatory qualification, so the same subject serves two sciences.
The aspect (ḥaythiyya) is crucial: it indicates the type of essential attribute being investigated.
4
Understand the definitions of the two sciences
The commentator defines ʿilm al-samāʾ as the science discussing bodies in general (heavenly and elemental) and their states, and ʿilm ṭabīʿī as the science discussing sensible bodies in terms of change and stability. Note the subjects and attributes.
These definitions reinforce the point that the same celestial bodies are studied from different perspectives.
5
Reflect on the theoretical consideration
The commentator raises a potential issue: whether the aspect is part of the subject or merely explanatory. He does not resolve it, indicating an ongoing scholarly debate. This shows the depth of analysis in usul al-fiqh.
This consideration does not undermine the main argument but adds nuance.

دعوی اول کی دلیل
اما الجواز : ۔ بے شارع نے پہلے دعوی کی دلیل کو واضح کیا ہے کہ ایسا ہوسکتا ہے کہ ئی واحد کیلئے عوارض ذاتیہ کی مختلف انواع ہوں ایک علم میں عوارض ذاتیہ کی ایک نوع سے بحث ہو اور دوسرے علم میں دوسری نوع سے بحث ہو اور ان میں امتیاز عوارض ذاتیہ کی وجہ سے ہو اور اس بات کی دلیل کہ علوم کا اتحاد و اختلاف مسائل کے اتحاد اور اختلاف پرمبنی ہے اگر مسائل مختلف ہوں تو علوم مختلف ہیں کیونکہ موضوع سے جیسے اختلاف علوم ثابت ہوتا ہے ایسے ہی محمول سے بھی اختلاف علوم ثابت ہوتا ہے۔ مسائل موضوع کی طرف راجع ہیں تو ایک علم ہے اسی طرح اگر تمام مسائل عوارض ذاتیہ کی ایک نوع کی طرف راجع ہوں تو بھی ایک علم ہوگا۔
دعوی ثانی کی دلیل
اما الوقوع سے دوسرے دعوی کی دلیل دی ہے کہ خارج میں شئی واحد ذاتا واعتبارا علوم کا موضوع بن رہی ہے حکماء نے تصریح کی ہے کہ علم ہیئت کا موضوع اجسام عالم من حیث الشکل نہیں اور علم السماء کا موضوع اجسام عالم من حیث الطبع ہیں ان دونوں علوم میں حیثیت بیان ہے عوارض ذاتیہ کیلئے کیونکہ ان میں نفس حیثیت سے بحث ہوتی ہے تو معلوم ہوا کہ حیثیت بیان
ہے جو نہیں ہے موضوع کا ورنہ اس حیثیت سے بحث نہ ہوتی تو حیثیت دونوں علوم میں موضوع سے خارج ہے تو دونوں کا موضوع اجسام عالم بنے باقی من حیث الشکل وغیرہ یہ بیان ہے موضوع میں داخل نہیں ہے اب اجسام عالم ذاتا واعتبارا امر واحد ہیں اور موضوع بن رہے ہیں دو علوم کا اور امتیاز عوارض کے اعتبار سے ہے کہ علم ہیئت میں اجسام عالم سے بحث شکل کے اعتبار سے ہوگی اور علم السماء میں اجسام عالم سے بحث طبائع کے اعتبار سے ہوگی تو امتیاز بھی دونوں علوم کے درمیان ہو گیا۔
علم السماء وعلم طبیعی کی تعریف اور موضوع کا بیان
علم السماء: ماقبل میں متن کی وضاحت تھی اب علم السماء س شارٹ نے علم اسماء العالم کی تعریف کی اورعلم السماء العالم کے مقسم یعنی علم طبعی کی تعریف اور علم طبعی کا موضوع بیان کیا ہے چونکہ ان کا ذکر متن میں تھا اسی مناسبت سے ذکر کر رہے ہیں۔ تعریف یہ ہے کہ علم السماء وہ علم ہے کہ جس میں اجسام کے احوال سے بحث کی جاتی ہے آگے عام ہے اجسام ارکان کے قبیل سے ہوں یعنی اجسام فلکیہ وغیرہ یا عناصر کے قبیل سے ہوں یعنی آگ پانی مٹی ہو اوغیرہ اجسام عالم سے بحث کی جاتی ہے کہ کس کرہ کی کیا کیفیت ہے؟ اور اس میں اجسام کی حکمت پیدائش کے اعتبار سے بیان کی جاتی ہے۔ نیز یہ بیان کیا جاتا ہے کہ اجسام کو تہہ بہ رکھا ہے سب سے نیچے ماء کا ہے پھر ارض ہے پھر ہوا ہے پھر آگ ہے اس کی حکمت کیا ہے اور علم طبیعی کی تعریف کی کہ علم طبعی ایسا علم ہے کہ جس میں اجسام سے بحث کی جاتی ہے من حیث اتغیر کہ کون سا جسم متحرک ہے؟ کونسا
ساکن ہے؟ نیز کتنی حرکت کرتا ہے اور علم طبعی کا موضوع وہ جسم محسوس ہے جو تغیر اور ثابت رہنے کیلئے پیش کیا گیا ہو۔
وَهَهُنَا نَظَرٌ أَمَّا أَوَّلًا فَلَّانَّ هَذَا مَبْنِيٌّ عَلَى مَا ذَكَرَ مِنْ كَوْنِ الْحَيْنِيَّةِ تَارَةً جُزْءًا من المَوْضُوعِ وَأُخْرَى بَيَانًا لِلْمَبُحُوثِ عَنْهَا وَقَدْ عَرَفْت مَا فِيهِ ، أَمَّا ثَانِيَا فَلَانَّهُمُ لَمَّا حَاوَلُوا مَعْرِفَةَ أَحْوَالِ الْأعْيَانِ الْمَوْجُودَاتِ وَضَعُوا لِلْحَقَائِقِ أَنْوَاعًا وَأَجْنَاسًا وَبَحَثُوا عَمَّا أَحَاطُوا بِهِ مِنْ أَعْرَاضِهِ الذَّاتِيَّةِ فَحَصَلَتْ لَهُمْ مَسَائِلُ كَثِيرَةٌ مُتَّحِدَةٌ فِي كَوْنِهَا بَحُثًا عَنْ أَحْوَالِ ذَلِكَ الْمَوْضُوعِ ، وَإِنْ اخْتَلَفَتْ مَحْمُوَلَاتُهَا فَجَعَلُوهَا بهَذَا الاعْتِبَارِ عِلْمًا وَاحِدًا يُفْرَدُ بِالتَّدْوينِ وَالتَّسْمِيَةِ وَجَوِّرُوا لِكُلِّ أَحَدٍ أَنْ يُضِيفَ إِلَيْهِ مَا يَطَّلِعُ عَلَيْهِ مِنْ أَحْوَالِ ذَلِكَ الْمَوْضُوعِ فَإِنَّ الْمُعْتَبَرَ فِي الْعِلْمِ هُوَ الْبَحْتُ عَنْ جَمِيعِ مَا تُحِيطُ بِهِ الطَّاقَةُ الْإِنْسَانِيَّةُ مِنْ الْأَعْرَاضِ الذَّاتِيَّةِ لِلْمَوْضُوعِ فَلَا مَعْنَى
The matn has two claims. Elaboration: His statement 'including the famous': The commentator criticizes the matn author that just as he opposed the people (qawm) regarding multiple subjects for one science, similarly in this third discussion he opposed them. The people say that a single thing in essence and consideration cannot be the subject of two sciences. But the author said that a single thing in essence and consideration can be the subject of more than one science, rather it actually occurs. Summary: there are two claims: (1) it is possible in essence and consideration for one thing to be subject to two sciences; (2) externally, one thing is actually serving as subject for two sciences. Proof of first claim: As to possibility: The commentator clarified the proof for the first claim: It is possible for one thing to have different types of essential attributes; one science discusses one type of essential attributes and another science discusses another type, and distinction between them is by the essential attributes. The proof for this is that the unity and diversity of sciences is based on the unity and diversity of their issues. If issues are different, sciences are different. Just as difference in sciences is established through the subject, similarly difference is established through the predicate. If issues revert to the subject, it is one science; likewise if all issues revert to one type of essential attributes, it is one science. Proof of second claim: As to actual occurrence: He gave proof for the second claim that externally a single thing in essence and consideration is serving as subject for sciences. The philosophers have stated that the subject of astronomy (hay'a) is the celestial bodies in terms of shape, and the subject of the science of the heavens (sama') is the celestial bodies in terms of nature. In both sciences, the aspect (haythiyya) is an explanation of the essential attributes, because discussion occurs in terms of that very aspect. So it is known that the aspect is not part of the subject; otherwise discussion would not occur from that aspect. So the aspect is external to the subject in both sciences. So the subject of both is the celestial bodies. The remaining matter such as shape etc. is explanation, not entered in the subject. Now the celestial bodies in essence and consideration are one thing and are serving as subject for two sciences, and distinction is by the attributes: in astronomy discussion of celestial bodies is in terms of shape, and in the science of the heavens discussion of celestial bodies is in terms of natures. So distinction between the two sciences occurs. Definition of 'ilm al-sama' and 'ilm tabi'i and statement of their subjects: 'Ilm al-sama': Previously there was clarification of the matn; now the commentator (sharih) defines 'ilm al-sama' and defines its genus, i.e., 'ilm tabi'i, and states the subject of 'ilm tabi'i. Since they were mentioned in the matn, accordingly he mentions them. The definition is that 'ilm al-sama' is the science in which discussion of bodies occurs generally - whether from the category of pillars (arkan) i.e., heavenly bodies etc., or from the category of elements i.e., fire, water, earth etc. The celestial bodies are discussed: what is the quality of each sphere? And the wisdom of creation of bodies is stated. Also it is stated that bodies are layered: water at bottom, then earth, then air, then fire - what is the wisdom? And the definition of 'ilm tabi'i: it is the science in which discussion of bodies occurs in terms of change - which body is moving? Which is stationary? Also how much motion it has. And the subject of 'ilm tabi'i is the sensible body that is prepared for change and stability. And here there is a consideration: Firstly, because this is built upon what he mentioned about the aspect (haythiyya) sometimes being part of the subject and sometimes being an explanation of what is discussed - and you know what is in that. Secondly, because when they attempted to know the states of existing entities, they placed types and genera for realities and investigated what they encompassed of their essential attributes. So they obtained many issues united in being a discussion of the states of that subject, even if their predicates differ. So they made them, by this consideration, one science singled out by recording and naming. And they allowed anyone to add to it what he discovers of the states of that subject. For the criterion of a science is the investigation of all that human capacity encompasses of the essential attributes of the subject. So it has no meaning.
Source pages p. 414

Commentary
This passage is a discussion from the commentary on Al-Tawdih wa al-Talwih addressing the philosophical question of whether a single entity can serve as the subject matter of multiple sciences. The commentator begins by stating that the matn author made two claims contrary to the prevailing view (qawm): first, that it is possible in essence for one thing to be the subject of multiple sciences; second, that this actually occurs in reality. The argument for possibility rests on the idea that sciences are distinguished by their types of essential attributes (ʿawāriḍ dhātiyya). If one thing has multiple types of essential attributes, different sciences can discuss different types. The unity or multiplicity of sciences depends on whether the issues (masāʾil) revert to one type of essential attribute or multiple. The argument for actuality uses the example of astronomy (ʿilm al-hayʾa) and the science of the heavens (ʿilm al-samāʾ). According to the philosophers, both sciences take celestial bodies (ajsām al-ʿālam) as their subject, but astronomy considers them in terms of shape (shakl) while the science of the heavens considers them in terms of nature (ṭabʿ). The aspect (ḥaythiyya) is not part of the subject but an explanatory qualification. Thus, the same celestial bodies serve as the subject for two sciences, distinguished by the type of attribute investigated. The commentator then provides definitions of these sciences and their subjects. He notes a potential theoretical concern about whether the aspect is part of the subject or merely explanatory, but he does not resolve it here. The passage is significant for usul al-fiqh because it establishes the principle that a single subject can be examined from multiple perspectives, which relates to how different Islamic sciences (e.g., fiqh, usul, kalam) can have overlapping subject matters (like human actions) while remaining distinct disciplines.

Key Terms
علم (ʿilm): A body of knowledge consisting of issues (masāʾil) discussing the essential attributes of a subject.
موضوع (mawḍūʿ): The entity whose essential attributes are investigated in a science.
عارض ذاتی (ʿāriḍ dhātī): An attribute that necessarily follows the essence of a thing and is directly discussed in a science (as opposed to accidental attributes).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What two claims does the matn author make about a single thing being the subject of multiple sciences?
A) It is impossible in principle but can occur by convention.
B) It is possible in principle and actually occurs in reality.
C) It is possible in principle but never occurs in reality.
D) It is impossible in principle and does not occur.

Key Takeaway
A single entity can be the subject of multiple sciences if each science investigates a distinct type of its essential attributes, as demonstrated by the example of astronomy and the science of the heavens.

Criteria for Distinguishing Sciences in Hanafi Usul
Critical analysis of science definition and differentiation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the criteria for distinguishing sciences in Hanafi usul and evaluate the three objections raised by the commentator against previous definitions.

Core Idea
A science is defined by its subject's essential accidents; sciences differ by subject essence or consideration, not by predicates.

This passage from the commentary on Al-Tawdih and Al-Talwih engages in a critical refinement of how sciences are defined and distinguished. The author first presents astronomy and physics as examples, then raises three objections (nazar) that challenge earlier views on the role of 'perspective' (al-haythiyya) in demarcating disciplines. Understanding this critique is essential for grasping how Hanafi usul scholars conceive the boundaries of fiqh and other sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim about science definition
Extract the author's core thesis: a single science investigates all essential accidents of a subject. The subject must be known and evident; the states (ahwal) are unknown and sought.
The subject is the foundation; the science aims to discover its essential accidents.
2
Analyze the example of astronomy vs. physics
Both sciences study celestial bodies, but astronomy focuses on shape (shakl) and physics on natures (taba'i'). This shows that the same subject can be studied under different considerations (i'tibar).
The distinction is not by subject per se but by the aspect under which it is examined.
3
List the three objections (nazar)
First objection: the concept of 'haythiyya' (perspective) is inconsistently used—sometimes part of the subject, sometimes an explanation. Second: when scholars investigate existent beings, they categorize them and then study their essential accidents, resulting in a unified science even if predicates differ; the key is the subject, not the variety of accidents studied. Third: if we treat different essential accidents as separate sciences, then any science can be arbitrarily subdivided (e.g., fiqh into a science of obligation, prohibition, etc.), leading to infinite division.
The third objection is particularly powerful: it shows that the unity of a science is not based on the number of accidents but on the subject itself.
4
Synthesize the correct criterion
From the objections, derive that sciences are distinguished by the subject being different in essence or by consideration (absolute vs. restricted). The subject is the cause of differentiation; the essential accidents are the objects of investigation but do not define the science's boundaries.
This aligns with the Hanafi view that fiqh is a single science despite covering multiple rulings.
5
Locate the reference to Ibn Sina
The author ends by noting that the verification of these discussions is in Ibn Sina's Book of Demonstration from the Logic of al-Shifa. This situates the critique within the broader Islamic philosophical tradition.
This indicates the interdisciplinary nature of usul al-fiqh methodology.

الترويحُ لِحَلَّ التَّوْضِيح وَالتَّلويح
ہے جو نہیں ہے موضوع کا ورنہ اس حیثیت سے بحث نہ ہوتی تو حیثیت دونوں علوم میں موضوع سے خارج ہے تو دونوں کا موضوع اجسام عالم بنے باقی من حیث الشکل وغیرہ یہ بیان ہے موضوع میں داخل نہیں ہے اب اجسام عالم ذاتا واعتبارا امر واحد ہیں اور موضوع بن رہے ہیں دو علوم کا اور امتیاز عوارض کے اعتبار سے ہے کہ علم ہیئت میں اجسام عالم سے بحث شکل کے اعتبار سے ہوگی اور علم السماء میں اجسام عالم سے بحث طبائع کے اعتبار سے ہوگی تو امتیاز بھی دونوں علوم کے درمیان ہو گیا۔ علم السماء وعلم طبیعی کی تعریف اور موضوع کا بیان
علم السماء: ماقبل میں متن کی وضاحت تھی اب علم السماء س شارٹ نے علم اسماء العالم کی تعریف کی اورعلم السماء العالم کے مقسم یعنی علم طبعی کی تعریف اور علم طبعی کا موضوع بیان کیا ہے چونکہ ان کا ذکر متن میں تھا اسی مناسبت سے ذکر کر رہے ہیں۔ تعریف یہ ہے کہ علم السماء وہ علم ہے کہ جس میں اجسام کے احوال سے بحث کی جاتی ہے آگے عام ہے اجسام ارکان کے قبیل سے ہوں یعنی اجسام فلکیہ وغیرہ یا عناصر کے قبیل سے ہوں یعنی آگ پانی مٹی ہو اوغیرہ اجسام عالم سے بحث کی جاتی ہے کہ کس کرہ کی کیا کیفیت ہے؟ اور اس میں اجسام کی حکمت پیدائش کے اعتبار سے بیان کی جاتی ہے۔ نیز یہ بیان کیا جاتا ہے کہ اجسام کو تہہ بہ رکھا ہے سب سے نیچے ماء کا ہے پھر ارض ہے پھر ہوا ہے پھر آگ ہے اس کی حکمت کیا ہے اور علم طبیعی کی تعریف کی کہ علم طبعی ایسا علم ہے کہ جس میں اجسام سے بحث کی جاتی ہے من حیث اتغیر کہ کون سا جسم متحرک ہے؟ کونسا
ساکن ہے؟ نیز کتنی حرکت کرتا ہے اور علم طبعی کا موضوع وہ جسم محسوس ہے جو تغیر اور ثابت رہنے کیلئے پیش کیا گیا ہو۔ وَهَهُنَا نَظَرٌ أَمَّا أَوَّلًا فَلَّانَّ هَذَا مَبْنِيٌّ عَلَى مَا ذَكَرَ مِنْ كَوْنِ الْحَيْنِيَّةِ تَارَةً جُزْءًا من المَوْضُوعِ وَأُخْرَى بَيَانًا لِلْمَبُحُوثِ عَنْهَا وَقَدْ عَرَفْت مَا فِيهِ ، أَمَّا ثَانِيَا فَلَانَّهُمُ لَمَّا حَاوَلُوا مَعْرِفَةَ أَحْوَالِ الْأعْيَانِ الْمَوْجُودَاتِ وَضَعُوا لِلْحَقَائِقِ أَنْوَاعًا وَأَجْنَاسًا وَبَحَثُوا عَمَّا أَحَاطُوا بِهِ مِنْ أَعْرَاضِهِ الذَّاتِيَّةِ فَحَصَلَتْ لَهُمْ مَسَائِلُ كَثِيرَةٌ مُتَّحِدَةٌ فِي كَوْنِهَا بَحُثًا عَنْ أَحْوَالِ ذَلِكَ الْمَوْضُوعِ ، وَإِنْ اخْتَلَفَتْ مَحْمُوَلَاتُهَا فَجَعَلُوهَا بهَذَا الاعْتِبَارِ عِلْمًا وَاحِدًا يُفْرَدُ بِالتَّدْوينِ وَالتَّسْمِيَةِ وَجَوِّرُوا لِكُلِّ أَحَدٍ أَنْ يُضِيفَ إِلَيْهِ مَا يَطَّلِعُ عَلَيْهِ مِنْ أَحْوَالِ ذَلِكَ الْمَوْضُوعِ فَإِنَّ الْمُعْتَبَرَ فِي الْعِلْمِ هُوَ الْبَحْتُ عَنْ جَمِيعِ مَا تُحِيطُ بِهِ الطَّاقَةُ الْإِنْسَانِيَّةُ مِنْ الْأَعْرَاضِ الذَّاتِيَّةِ لِلْمَوْضُوعِ فَلَا مَعْنَى
لِلعِلم الْوَاحِدِ إلَّا أن يُوضَعَ شَيْءٍ أَوْ أَشْيَاء مُتَنَاسِبَةٌ فَنَبْحَثَ عَنْ جَمِيعِ عَوَارِضِهِ الذَّاتِيَّةِ وَنَطْلُبَهَا وَلَا مَعْنَى لِتَمَايُزِ الْعُلُومِ إِلَّا أَنَّ هَذَا يَنظُرُ فِي أَحْوَالِ شَيْءٍ وَذَاكَ فِي أَخْوَالِ شَيْءٍ آخَرَ مُغَابِرَ لَهُ بِالذَّاتِ أَوْ بِالِاعْتِبَارِ بِأَنْ يُؤْخَذَ فِي أَحَدِ الْعِلْمَيْنِ مُطْلَقًا ، وَفِي الْآخَرِ مُقَيْدًا أَوْ يُؤخَذَ فِي كُلِّ مِنْهُمَا مُقَيَّدًا بِقَيْدٍ آخَرَ وَتِلْكَ الأَحْوَالُ مَجْهُولَةٌ مَطْلُوبَةٌ وَالْمَوْضُوعُ مَعْلُومٌ بَيْنَ الْوُجُودِ فَهُوَ الصَّالِحُ سَبَبًا لِلسَّمَائِزِ . وَأَمَّا ثَالِثًا فَلَانَّهُ مَا مِنْ عِلْمٍ إِلَّا وَيَشْتَمِلُ مَوْضُوعُهُ عَلَى أَعْرَاضٍ ذَاتِيَّةٍ مُتَنَوِّعَةٍ فَلِكُلّ أَحَدٍ أَنْ يَجْعَلَهُ عُلُومًا مُتَعَدِّدَةً بِهَذَا الاعْتِبَارِ مَثَلًا يَجْعَلُ الْبَحْث عَنْ فِعل المُكَلَّفِ مِنْ حَيْثُ الوُجُوبُ عِلْمًا وَمِنْ حَيْثُ الْحُرُمَةِ عِلْمًا آخَرَ إِلَى غَيْرِ ذَلِكَ فَيَكُونُ الْفِقْهُ عُلُومًا مُتَعَدِّدَةً مَوْضُوعُهَا فِعْلُ الْمُكَلَّفِ فَلَا يَنْضَبِطُ الاتِّحَادُ
وَالاخْتِلافُ وَتَحْقِيقُ هَذِهِ الْمَبَاحِثِ فِي كِتَاب الْبُرُهَان مِنْ مَنطِقِ الشَّفَاء ..
ترجمہ: اور یہاں نظر ہے بہر حال اولا کیونکہ مبنی ہے اس پر جس کو ذکر کیا ہے کہ ہوتی ہے حیثیت کبھی جز موضوع کا اور کبھی بیان مجوث عنہا کا اور تحقیق تو نے پہچان لیا اس کو جو اس میں (خرابی) ہے بہر حال ثانیا کیونکہ جب وہ ارادہ کرتے ہیں اعیان موجودات کے احوال کی معرفت کا تو وہ وضع کرتے ہیں حقیقتوں کیلئے انواع اور جنسیں اور بحث کرتے ہیں اس سے جس کے ذریعے وہ احاطہ کر لیں اس کے اعراض ذاتیہ کا پس حاصل ہوتے ہیں ان کو ایسے مسائل کثیرہ جو متحد ہوتے ہیں ان کے بحث ہونے میں اس موضوع سے اگر چہ مختلف ہوں ان (مسائل) کے معمولات پر بناتے ہیں وہ اس اعتبار سے ایک علم منفرد کرتے ہیں مدون کرنے میں اور نام رکھنے میں اور جائز قرار دیتے ہیں ہر ایک کیلئے کہ نسبت کر دے وہ اس کی طرف اس چیز کی جس پر مطلع ہو وہ اس موضوع کے احوال میں سے کیونکہ معتبر علم میں وہ بحث کرتا ہے تمام ان چیزوں سے جن کا طاقت انسانی احاطہ کر سکتی ہے موضوع کے اعراض ذاتیہ میں سے پس نہیں ہے معنی علم واحد کا مگر یہ کہ وضع کیا جائے ایک شئی کو یا کئی اشیاء کو جو متناسب ہوں پس بحث کی جائے اس کی تمام عوارض ذاتیہ سے اور طلب کیا جائے ان کو اور نہیں ہے معنی تمایز علوم کا مگر یہی کہ نظر کی جائے ایک شئی کے احوال میں اور وہ دوسری شئی کے احوال میں مغایر ہو اس کے بالذات یا بالاعتبار بایں طور پر کہ
And here there is a consideration: Firstly, because this is based on what was mentioned of 'al-haythiyya' sometimes being part of the subject and sometimes an explanation of what is being investigated, and you have already known what is in it. Secondly, because when they attempted to know the states of the existent entities, they formulated for the realities types and genera, and investigated what they encompassed of its essential accidents. Thus there resulted for them many issues united in being an investigation of the states of that subject, even if their predicates differed. So they made it, by this consideration, one science, singled out for codification and naming. And they allowed anyone to add to it what he discovers of the states of that subject. For what is considered in a science is the investigation of all that human capability encompasses of the essential accidents of the subject. So there is no meaning for a single science except that a thing or things that are proportionate be posited, and then we investigate all its essential accidents and seek them. And there is no meaning for the differentiation of sciences except that this one looks into the states of a thing and that one into the states of another thing that is different from it in essence or by consideration, such that it is taken in one science absolutely and in the other restrictively, or taken in each restrictively with another restriction. And those states are unknown and sought, while the subject is known and evident in existence, so it is suitable as a cause for differentiation. Thirdly, because there is no science but that its subject includes various essential accidents, so anyone can make it multiple sciences by this consideration. For example, he can make the investigation of the act of the obligated person from the perspective of obligation a science, and from the perspective of prohibition another science, and so on. Thus fiqh would be multiple sciences whose subject is the act of the obligated person. So unity and difference are not regulated. And the verification of these discussions is in the Book of Demonstration from the Logic of al-Shifa.
Source pages p. 415

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis of how sciences are defined and distinguished, coming from the commentary on Al-Tawdih and Al-Talwih. The author first presents a definition of astronomy ('ilm al-sama') and physics ('ilm tabi'i) as examples, explaining their subjects and perspectives. Then he raises three objections (nazar) concerning the criteria for distinguishing sciences. First, he criticizes the earlier claim that 'al-haythiyya' (perspective) can sometimes be part of the subject and sometimes an explanation of what is investigated. Second, he argues that when scholars investigate existent beings, they categorize them and study their essential accidents, and the resulting sciences are unified by the subject even if predicates differ. He insists that a single science is defined by investigating all essential accidents of a subject, not by a limited set. Third, he points out that if we take different essential accidents of the same subject as different sciences, then any science can be subdivided arbitrarily, leading to infinite multiplication. For example, fiqh could be divided into a science of obligation, a science of prohibition, etc. The author refers to Ibn Sina's Book of Demonstration for further verification. The commentary thus refines the definition of science and emphasizes that differentiation is based on the subject itself, either in essence or by consideration (e.g., absolute vs. restricted), and the states are unknown and sought.

Key Terms
الموضوع (al-mawḍūʿ): The subject matter of a science; the thing under investigation, which is known and evident in existence.
العرض الذاتي (al-ʿaraḍ al-dhātī): An essential accident inherent to the subject of a science, which the science seeks to investigate. It is the 'mahmul' (predicate) that the science studies.
الاعتبار (al-iʿtibār): The perspective or aspect under which something is considered in a science. For example, celestial bodies can be considered from the perspective of shape (hay'a) or nature (tabi'a).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary basis for distinguishing different sciences?
A) The number of questions answered
B) The subject matter being different in essence or by consideration (absolute/restricted)
C) The practical utility of the science
D) The fame of the scholars who founded them

Key Takeaway
Sciences are distinguished by their subject being different in essence or by consideration (absolute vs. restricted), not by the variety of essential accidents studied; this prevents arbitrary fragmentation.

Method of Learning
advanced
12 min
Objections on the Basis for Distinguishing Sciences: Subject, Predicate, or Aspect?
Author presents and answers two objections regarding basis for distinguishing sciences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two objections raised against basing the distinction of sciences on subject matter or on predicates, and explain the Hanafi response to each objection.

Core Idea
The subject matter (mawḍūʿ) is the only stable basis for distinguishing sciences; aspects (ḥaythiyya) are merely explicative, and predicates are too numerous.

How do we tell one science apart from another? In the logical classification of the sciences, the Hanafī position is that the subject matter—the primary entity studied—is what defines a science. However, a commentator of at‑Tawḍīḥ raises two sharp objections: first, that if we consider the aspect (ḥaythiyya) as a qualifier, then the same thing can be the subject of multiple sciences; second, that distinction could instead be based on the predicates (essential attributes). In this lesson we will examine these objections and the Hanafī responses, which together sharpen our understanding of how a discipline is constituted and bounded.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two contested positions
Read the passage and note that the dispute is about what makes one science different from another. The author of at‑Tawḍīḥ allowed distinction by predicates and also argued that a single thing can be the subject of multiple sciences through different aspects. The commentator (shārīḥ) rejects both and insists that distinction must be based on the subject matter.
The word 'aspect' (ḥaythiyya) is central: is it a qualifier that changes the subject, or just an explicative description?
2
Analyse the first objection and its response
The first objection says: if 'from the aspect of form' and 'from the aspect of nature' are qualifiers, then the subject differs and a single thing cannot be the subject of two sciences. The response is that treating the aspect as a qualifier ultimately reverts to it being an explicative—the subject remains the same, only considered under different descriptions.
The commentator's own argument that the aspect is a qualifier is turned on its head: it reduces to the explicative, so the original claim stands.
3
Analyse the second objection and its response
The second objection gives three reasons why distinction cannot be based on predicates: (a) philosophers unify problems under one subject; (b) the definition of distinction requires subjects to differ; (c) attributes are too many and unknown. The response counters that attributes are known in themselves—only their relation to the subject is unknown, and that does not prevent them from being a basis. Note that the response here is partially from the author of at‑Tawḍīḥ, but the commentator presents it as a rebuttal.
This objection is actually a defence of the subject‑based view; the author of at‑Tawḍīḥ seems to allow predicate‑based distinction, and the commentator refutes it.
4
Synthesise the Hanafī position
From the two objections and responses, extract the core Hanafī stance: a science is defined by its subject matter (mawḍūʿ); essential attributes are studied but do not constitute the identity of the science; aspects are merely explanatory and do not create new subjects.
This principle is applied in later Hanafī works when defining the scope of uṣūl al‑fiqh, fiqh, kalām, etc.

Source Text

الترويح لحل التوضيح والتلويح: يؤخذ من علمين من حيث الإطلاق والتقييد أو يؤخذ كل منهما مقيدا بقيد آخر، وتلك الأحوال مجهولة والمطلوب معلوم، والموضوع معلوم الوجود، فهو صالح للتمييز، وبكل حال ثالثا: لا علم إلا ومشتمل على عوارض ذاتية، فلكل أن يجعلها علومًا متعددة. مثال: فعل المكلف من حيث الوجوب علم، ومن حيث الحرمة علم آخر، فتكون فقه علوم متعددة وموضوعها فعل المكلف. فلا ينضبط الاتحاد والاختلاف. وتحقيق مباحثها في كتاب البرهان في المنطق.

هل الحيطة تكون قيدا للموضوع؟

ههنا نظر: من هنا شرع الشارح في ثلاثة اعتراضات.

الاعتراض الأول: حاصله أن صاحب التوضيح استدل على أن الشيء الواحد قد يكون موضوعا لعلوم متعددة بأن الحكماء صرحوا بأن علم الهيئة موضوعه الأجسام من حيث الشكل، وعلم السماء والعالم موضوعه الأجسام من حيث الطبيعة، فالشيء الواحد موضوع لعلمين. لكن الحيطة بيانية وخارجة عن الموضوع. فقال الشارح: ليست الحيطة بيانية بل قيد. فـ(من حيث الشكل) و(من حيث الطبيعة) قيدان للموضوع، فيختلف الموضوع باعتبار القيود، فلا يكون الشيء الواحد موضوعا لعلوم متعددة.

الجواب: إن تفسير الحيطة بالقيد يرجع إلى معنى الحيطة البيانية كما مر.

هل يمكن جعل تمييز العلوم مداره على المحمولات؟

الاعتراض الثاني: حاصله أن صاحب التوضيح قال إن تمييز العلوم قد يكون بالمحمولات. فقال الشارح: لا يصح جعل تمييز العلوم مداره على المحمولات لوجوه:

الأول: أن الحكماء حين أرادوا معرفة أحوال الأعيان (الموجودات الخارجية) عينوا الحقائق (أجناسها وأنواعها) ثم أثبتوا عوارضها الذاتية، فحصلت مسائل كثيرة متحدة بحسب الموضوعات مختلفة بحسب المحمولات. فجعلوا تلك المسائل الكثيرة علما واحدا باعتبار وحدة الموضوع، وأذنوا لمن بعده أن يدخل فيه ما يعلم من عوارض ذاتية لذلك الموضوع. فاتحادهم للمسائل الكثيرة في علم واحد إنما هو لوحدة الموضوع.

الثاني: أن معنى تمييز العلوم يقتضي أن يكون المدار على الموضوعات، لأن التمييز أن تجعل شيئا واحدا أو أشياء متناسبة موضوعا لعلم وتذكر عوارضه الذاتية، وشيئا آخر موضوعا لعلم آخر وتذكر عوارضه الذاتية. فالموضوعان إما متغايران ذاتا أو اعتبارا. مثال التغاير الذاتي: موضوع الفقه فعل المكلف، وموضوع الهندسة المقدار. ومثال التغاير الاعتباري: إما أن يكون موضوع أحد العلمين مطلقا ذاتا واعتبارا والآخر مقيدا، أو يكون موضوع كل منهما مقيدا بقيدين مختلفين. فالمدار على الموضوع لا على العوارض الذاتية.

الثالث: أن الموضوع معلوم والعوارض الذاتية مجهولة لكثرتها، فالتمييز يحتاج إلى سبب معلوم، والموضوع معلوم، والعوارض مجهولة، فكيف يكون المدار عليها؟

الجواب: الاعتراض يشتمل على جزئين:
(1) أن الحكماء اعتبروا الموضوع. وهذا عين محل النزاع فلا يصلح دليلا.
(2) أن التمييز يجب أن يكون بمعلوم. والجواب أن العوارض الذاتية في نفسها معلومة، وإنما نسبت إلى الموضوع مجهولة. والنسبة المجهولة لا تؤثر. فالعوارض معلومة كالموضوع، فيصح جعلها مدارا للتمييز.

سؤال: ادعينا أن الموضوع معلوم والعوارض مجهولة، وليس هذا مطلقا بل بالنسبة إلى الشارع في العلم...
At-Tarwīḥ li-Ḥall at-Tawḍīḥ wa at-Talwīḥ: It is taken from two sciences in terms of absoluteness and restriction, or each is taken as restricted with another restriction, and those states are unknown while what is sought is known, and the subject matter is known in existence, so it is capable of being a cause for distinction. And in any case, thirdly: There is no science except that it comprises essential attributes, so each person may turn them into multiple sciences. For example: The act of the obligated person from the aspect of obligation is a science, and from the aspect of prohibition is another science, so fiqh becomes multiple sciences with the act of the obligated person as their subject. Thus unity and diversity are not regulated. The investigation of its discussions is in the book al-Burhān on logic.

Can the aspect be a qualifier for the subject?

Here is an objection: From here the commentator began three objections.

The first objection: Its gist is that the author of at-Tawḍīḥ argued that one thing can be the subject of multiple sciences because the philosophers stated that the subject of astronomy is bodies from the aspect of form, and the subject of cosmology is the same bodies from the aspect of nature, so one thing is the subject of two sciences. But the aspect is explanatory and external to the subject. The commentator said: The aspect is not explanatory but a qualifier. Thus 'from the aspect of form' and 'from the aspect of nature' are qualifiers for the subject, so the subject differs by the qualifiers, and one thing cannot be the subject of multiple sciences.

Answer: Interpreting the aspect as a qualifier reverts to the meaning of the explanatory aspect, as mentioned.

Can the distinction of sciences be made dependent on predicates?

The second objection: Its gist is that the author of at-Tawḍīḥ said that the distinction of sciences may be by predicates. The commentator said: It is not correct to make the distinction of sciences dependent on predicates, for several reasons:

First: When the philosophers intended to know the states of entities (external existents), they determined the realities (their genera and species) and then established their essential attributes, so they obtained many problems that are united in terms of subject but differ in terms of predicates. They made those many problems a single science by virtue of the unity of subject, and they permitted those after them to include into it whatever essential attributes of that subject become known. Thus their unification of the many problems into one science was only due to the unity of subject.

Second: The meaning of distinction of sciences requires that the basis be subjects, because distinction is to make one thing or proportional things the subject of a science and mention its essential attributes, and another thing the subject of another science and mention its essential attributes. The two subjects are either essentially different or different by consideration. An example of essential difference: The subject of fiqh is the act of the obligated person, and the subject of geometry is magnitude. An example of difference by consideration: Either the subject of one science is absolute in essence and consideration and the other is qualified, or the subject of each is qualified with two different qualifiers. So the basis is the subject, not the essential attributes.

Third: The subject is known and the essential attributes are unknown due to their multiplicity, so distinction requires a known cause. The subject is known, the attributes are unknown, so how can they be the basis?

Answer: The objection comprises two parts:
(1) That the philosophers considered the subject. This is exactly the point of dispute, so it cannot serve as evidence.
(2) That distinction must be by something known. The answer is that the essential attributes are known in themselves; only their attribution to the subject is unknown. The unknown attribution does not affect. So the attributes are as known as the subject, and thus they can be the basis for distinction.

Question: We claimed that the subject is known and the attributes are unknown, but this is not absolute; rather for the commentator in the science...
Source pages p. 417

Commentary
This passage from At-Tarwīḥ li-Ḥall at-Tawḍīḥ wa at-Talwīḥ is a critical refinement where the commentator (shāriḥ) objects to positions taken by the author of At-Tawḍīḥ. The core issue is the epistemological basis for distinguishing one science from another: is it the subject matter (mawḍū‘), the predicates or essential attributes (ʿawāriḍ dhātiyya), or the aspect (haythiyya)? The commentator raises three objections (only two are fully presented in this window) and provides answers. The first objection challenges that a single thing can serve as subject for multiple sciences if the 'aspect' is considered a qualifier rather than an explicative. The commentator insists that if you treat 'from the aspect of form' and 'from the aspect of nature' as qualifiers, you effectively change the subject, so unity is lost. The answer, however, is that the qualifier interpretation collapses into the explicative interpretation—it does not create a genuinely new subject. The second objection argues that distinction cannot be based on predicates because: (a) philosophers unify problems under a single subject; (b) the very definition of scientific distinction presupposes different subjects; and (c) attributes are too numerous and unknown to serve as a reliable basis. The response counters that the attributes themselves are known; only their relation to the subject is unknown, and that does not impede distinction. The passage ends mid-sentence at the third objection, which will presumably be completed on the following page. This entire discussion is a theoretical exercise in the classification of sciences (taqsīm al-ʿulūm) as it relates to the foundations of fiqh and other disciplines, and it reflects the rigorous logical method of the later Hanafi school.

Key Terms
الموضوع (al-mawḍūʿ): The subject matter of a science; the primary entity whose essential attributes are studied.
العارض الذاتي (al-ʿāriḍ al-dhātī): An essential attribute of the subject, one that belongs to it by its very nature and is the focus of scientific inquiry.
الحيثية (al-ḥaythiyya): The aspect or respect under which a subject is considered; here debated as whether it is a qualifier (qayd) or explicative (bayāniyya).
تمایز العلوم (tamāyuz al-ʿulūm): The distinction among sciences, typically by subject matter, predicates, or aspects.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the fundamental reason why the commentator objects to basing the distinction of sciences on predicates?
A) Predicates are unknown in themselves
B) The philosophers never used predicates to distinguish sciences
C) The meaning of distinction requires different subjects, not different predicates
D) Predicates are only secondary to the subject

Key Takeaway
The Hanafī school defends that the subject matter is the only proper basis for distinguishing sciences; aspects are explicative, and predicates are studied within a science but do not define its identity.

Subject-Matter as the Basis for Distinguishing Sciences
Presents and defends the view that sciences are distinguished by subject-matter rather than essential attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principle that sciences are distinguished by their subject-matter (mawdu') rather than essential attributes to identify the mawdu' of a given science and evaluate objections.

Core Idea
The distinction of sciences is based on the subject-matter, which is known and provides clear demarcation.

In Islamic epistemology, particularly in usul al-fiqh, the question of how sciences are distinguished is foundational. This passage defends the view that the subject-matter (mawdu') is the primary basis, not the essential attributes ('awaridh dhatiyya). It presents two reasons and addresses objections from those who argue that essential attributes could also serve as the basis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two reasons
The author gives two reasons: (1) the very meaning of distinction implies different subjects; (2) the subject is known while attributes are unknown due to their multiplicity.
Reason two is presented after the first; look for the key phrases 'falam'ani lil-wahid' and 'tilka al-ahwal al-majhulah'.
2
Analyze the definition of distinction
Distinction means making a single thing or proportionate things the subject of a science and discussing its essential attributes. This definition inherently requires different subjects for different sciences.
The author illustrates with examples: fiqh's subject is the act of the mukallaf, geometry's subject is quantity.
3
Examine the first objection and response
An objection claims the first reason is the very point in dispute and that attributes could also be the basis. The author responds that attributes in themselves are known, their relation to subject is innate, so unknownness of relation doesn't affect.
The response distinguishes between attributes per se and their relation to the subject.
4
Consider the second objection about the founder
The objector says the founder of a science must know all attributes to distinguish. The author answers that knowing the subject suffices; it is enough that attributes can serve as a cause of distinction.
This refutes the idea that complete knowledge of attributes is required.
5
Apply the principle to a new science
Given a science like nahw (grammar) or sarf (morphology), identify its mawdu' from its definition. This practice reinforces the subject-based demarcation.
For example, nahw studies the Arabic word in terms of inflection (i'rab), so its mawdu' is the Arabic word (kalimah).

دوسری وجہ: فلامعنی للواحد سے بیان کی کہ تمایز علوم کا معنی بھی تقاضا کرتا ہے کہ تمایز کامدار موضوعات پر ہو کیونکہ تمایز علوم کا معنی یہ ہے کہ شئی واحد کو یا اشیاء متناسبہ کو کسی علم کا موضوع بنا کر اس کے عوارض ذاتیہ سے بحث کی جائے اور دوسری نشی کو دوسرے علم کا موضوع بنا کر اس کے عوارض ذاتیہ سے بحث کی جائے جو پہلی شئی کے مغایر ہو تو دو علموں کا موضوع دو چیزیں ہیں پھر موضوعین میں تقسیم ہے یا تو ان میں تغایر ذاتی ہوگا یا تغایر اعتباری ہوگا اگر تفاسیر ذاتی ہو اس کی مثال جیسے علم فقہ کا موضوع فعل مکلف ہے اور علم ہندسہ کا موضوع مقدار ہے اور اگر تغایر اعتباری ہو تو اس کی دو صورتیں ہیں (۱) ایک علم کا موضوع مطلق ہو ذاتا و اعتبارا اور دوسرے علم کا موضوع مقید ہو (۲) ایک علم کا موضوع مقید ہو ایک قید کے ساتھ اور دوسرے علم کا موضوع مقید ہو قید آخر سے تو پھر بھی مدار امتیاز موضوع ہو نہ کہ اعراض ذاتیہ۔ تیسری وجہ: تلک الاحوال مجہولہ سے بیان کیا کہ موضوع معلوم ہوتا ہے اور عوارض ذاتیہ بہت زیادہ ہونے کی وجہ سے مجہول ہوتے ہیں تو امتیاز بین العلوم کیلئے ایسا سبب ہونا چاہیے جو کہ معلوم ہو اور موضوع معلوم ہوتا ہے اور عوارض ذاتیہ بوجہ کثیر ہونے کے مجہول ہوتے ہیں تو پھر اعراض پر امتیاز بین العلوم کا مدار کیسے ہو سکتا ہے؟ جواب: یہ ہے کہ اعتراض کا حاصل دو چیزیں تھیں (۱) حکماء کا طریقہ کہ انہوں نے موضوع کا اعتبار کیا ہے (۲) تمایز علوم کا مدار معلوم چیز ہونی چاہئیے نہ کہ مجہول۔ اب سوال کا پہلا جز بعینہ محل نزاع ہے یہی تو جھگڑا ہے کہ علوم کے اختلاف کا سبب صرف موضوع ہے یا عوارض بھی ہیں لہذا اس کو دلیل بنانا درست نہیں ہے اور دوسرے جز کا جواب یہ ہے کہ ایک ہوتے ہیں اعراض ذاتیہ اور ایک ہے ان اعراض کی نسبت موضوع کی طرف کرنا عوارض فی نفسہ معلوم ہوتے ہیں ان کی موضوع کی طرف نسبت مجبول ہے اور نسبت کے مجہول ہو نے سے کوئی فرق نہیں پڑتا خلاصہ یہ ہے کہ جیسا کہ موضوع معلوم ہے ایسے ہی عوارض ذاتیہ بھی معلوم ہوتے ہیں تو اعراض پر تمایز علوم کا مدار ہوسکتا ہے۔ سوال: ہم نے کہا کہ موضوع معلوم ہیں اور عوارض مجبول ہیں یہ مطلقا نہیں بلکہ شارع فی العلم کیلئے موضوع کا معلوم ہونا ضروری ہے اور اعراض ذاتیہ سے وہ اس علم کو تب ممتاز کر سکتا ہے جب تمام عوارض کو جانتا ہو اب شارع فی العلم کیلئے تمام عوارض کا جانا مجود رہے ہم نے شارع فی العلم کے بارے میں کہا کہ عوارض ذاتیہ مجہول ہیں۔ جواب: شارع فی العلم کیلئے ان تمام تفصیلات کا جاننا ضروری نہیں بلکہ اتنا جانا کافی ہے کہ یہ امتیاز کا سبب بن سکیں۔
Second reason: From 'Falam'ani lil-wahid' it is said that the meaning of distinction of sciences also requires that the basis of distinction be subjects, because the meaning of distinction of sciences is that a single thing or proportionate things is made the subject of some science and its essential attributes are discussed, and another thing is made the subject of another science and its essential attributes are discussed, which is different from the first thing. So the subjects of two sciences are two things. Then the two subjects are either essentially different or conventionally different. If essentially different, an example is: the subject of fiqh is the act of the mukallaf, and the subject of geometry is quantity. If conventionally different, there are two forms: (1) the subject of one science is absolute both in essence and consideration, and the subject of the other is restricted; (2) the subject of one is restricted by one restriction and the subject of the other by another restriction. Then still the basis of distinction is the subject, not the essential attributes. Third reason: From 'Tilka al-ahwal al-majhulah' it is explained that the subject is known, and essential attributes due to being numerous are unknown. So for distinction between sciences, there must be a cause that is known. The subject is known, and essential attributes due to being numerous are unknown. So how can the basis of distinction between sciences be on attributes? Answer: The outcome of the objection is two things: (1) The method of the philosophers that they considered the subject; (2) The basis of distinction between sciences must be a known thing, not an unknown one. Now the first part of the question is exactly the point of dispute: the cause of difference of sciences is only the subject or also attributes? Hence taking it as a proof is not correct. The answer to the second part is: there are essential attributes, and there is the relation of these attributes to the subject. The attributes in themselves are known; their relation to the subject is innate; and the unknownness of the relation makes no difference. Summary: Just as the subject is known, so too essential attributes are known. So the basis of distinction between sciences can be on attributes. Question: We said that the subject is known and attributes are innate; this is not absolute, rather for the founder of the science, knowledge of the subject is necessary, and he can distinguish the science by essential attributes only when he knows all attributes. Now we said regarding the founder of the science that essential attributes are unknown. Answer: For the founder of the science, knowing all these details is not necessary; rather it is sufficient to know that they can be a cause of distinction.
Source pages p. 418

Commentary
This passage presents and defends the view that the distinction between sciences is based on subject-matter (mawdu') rather than essential attributes ('awaridh dhatiyya). The author gives two reasons: first, the very meaning of distinction of sciences implies that different subjects lead to different sciences; second, the subject is known while the essential attributes are unknown due to their multiplicity, so the basis must be the known subject. The author then responds to an objection that the first part is the very point of dispute and that the unknownness of attributes does not preclude them from being the basis. Finally, he addresses a further objection about the founder of the science needing to know all attributes, answering that it is sufficient to know that they serve as a cause of distinction.

Key Terms
موضوع (mawdu'): The subject-matter of a science, i.e., the primary object of study.
عوارض ذاتیہ (awaridh dhatiyyah): Essential attributes or properties that are intrinsic to the subject of a science.
تمایز علوم (tamayyuz al-'ulum): The distinction or differentiation between sciences.

Key Terms from Source Passage
